<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regions_of_colonial_Mexico.2C_centers_and_peripheries">Regions of colonial Mexico, centers and peripheries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Regions of colonial Mexico, centers and peripheries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the colonial period, basic patterns of regional development emerged and strengthened<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-provinces_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-provinces-42">[41]</a></sup> European settlement and institutional life was built on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesoamerican" title="Mesoamerican">Mesoamerican</a> heartland of the <a href="/wiki/Aztec_Empire" title="Aztec Empire">Aztec Empire</a> in Central Mexico. The South (Oaxaca, Michoacan, Yucatán, and Central America) was also in the region of dense indigenous settlement of Mesoamerica, but in the absence of exploitable resources of interest to Europeans, the South attracted few Europeans, while the indigenous presence remained strong. The North was outside region of complex indigenous populations, but with the discovery of silver in the region of nomadic and hostile northern indigenous groups, the Spanish sought to conquer or pacify those peoples in order to exploit the mines and develop enterprises to supply them. Since much of northern New Spain attracted few Europeans and had a sparse indigenous population, the Spanish crown and later the Republic of Mexico did not effectively exert sovereignty of the region, leaving it vulnerable to the expansionism of the United States in the nineteenth century.</p>
<p>Regional characteristics of colonial Mexico have been the focus of considerable scholarship within the vast scholarship on centers and peripheries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-provinces_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-provinces-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> For those based in vice-regal capital of Mexico City itself, anywhere else was the "provinces". Even in modern era, "Mexico" for many refers solely to Mexico City this pejorative view of anywhere but the capital is a hopeless backwater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> "Fuera de México, todo es Cuauhtitlán" ["outside of Mexico City, it's all Podunk"].<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> that is, poor, marginal, and backward, in short, the periphery. The picture is far more complex, however, so that while the capital is enormously important as the center of power of various kinds (institutional, economic, social), the provinces played a significant role in colonial Mexico. Regions (provinces) developed and thrived to the extent that they were sites of economic production and tied into networks of trade. "Spanish society in the Indies was import-export oriented at the very base and in every aspect," and the development of many regional economies was usually centered on support of that export sector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_region">Central region</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Central region">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexico_City.2C_Capital_of_the_Viceroyalty">Mexico City, Capital of the Viceroyalty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Mexico City, Capital of the Viceroyalty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Mexico_City" title="History of Mexico City">History of Mexico City</a></div>
<p>The center of the Central region was Mexico City. The <a href="/wiki/History_of_Mexico_City" title="History of Mexico City">development of Mexico City</a> itself is extremely important to the development of New Spain as a whole. It was the seat of the Viceroyalty of New Spain, the Archdiocese of the Catholic Church, the Holy Office of the <a href="/wiki/Inquisition" title="Inquisition">Inquisition</a>, the merchants' guild (<i>consulado</i>), and the most elite families in the Kingdom of New Spain. Mexico City was the single-most populous city, not just in New Spain, but for many years the entire Western Hemisphere, with a high concentration of mixed-race <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Castas" title="Castas">castas</a>. It was the hub of New Spain, the center of the center.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Veracruz_to_Mexico_City">Veracruz to Mexico City</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Veracruz to Mexico City">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The areas from the capital east to the port of Veracruz saw significant regional development along this main transportation route. <a href="/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt" title="Alexander von Humboldt">Alexander von Humboldt</a> called this area "Mesa de Anahuac" which can be defined as the adjacent valleys of Puebla, Mexico, and Toluca, enclosed by high mountains, along with their connections to the ports of Gulf Coast port of Veracruz and the Pacific port of <a href="/wiki/Acapulco" title="Acapulco">Acapulco</a>, where over half the population of New Spain lived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> These valleys were linked trunk lines, that is main routes, facilitating the movement of vital goods and people to get to key areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> But the difficulty of transit of people and goods, even in this relatively richly endowed region of Mexico in the absence of rivers and level terrain should not be discounted as a major challenge to the economy of New Spain. This challenge of transit persisted during the post-independence years until the late nineteenth-century construction of railroads. In the colonial era and up until railroads were built in key areas, mule trains were the main mode of transporting goods. Mules were used since unpaved roads and mountainous terrain could not generally accommodate carts.</p>
<p>In the late eighteenth century the crown devoted some resources to the study and remedy the problem of poor roads. The Camino Real (royal road) between the port of Veracruz and the capital had some short sections paved and bridges constructed. The construction was done despite protests from some Indian villages when the infrastructure improvements, which sometimes included rerouting the road through communal lands. The Spanish crown finally decided that road improvement was in the interests of the state for military purposes, as well as for fomenting commerce, agriculture, and industry, but the lack of state involvement in the development of physical infrastructure was to have lasting effects constraining development until the late nineteenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> Despite some improvements, the roads still made transit difficult, particularly for heavy military equipment.</p>
<p>Although the crown's plans for both the Toluca and Veracruz portions of the king's highway were ambitious, the actual results saw improvements only for a localized network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> Even where infrastructure was improved, transit on the Veracruz-Puebla main road had other obstacles, with wolves attacking mule trains, killing animals, and rendering some sacks of foodstuffs unsellable because they were smeared with blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> The north-south Acapulco route remained a mule track through mountainous terrain.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Veracruz.2C_port_city_and_province">Veracruz, port city and province</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Veracruz, port city and province">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Veracruz was the first Spanish settlement founded in what became New Spain, but it endured as the only viable Gulf Coast <a href="/wiki/Port" title="Port">port</a>, the enduring gateway for Spain to New Spain. But the difficult topography around the port itself affected not only local development, but also affected New Spain as a whole. Going from the port to the central plateau entailed a daunting 2000 meter climb from the narrow tropical coastal plain in over just a hundred kilometers. The narrow, slippery road in the mountain mists was treacherous for mule trains, and in some cases mules were hoisted by ropes. Many tumbled with their cargo to their deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> Given these transport constraints, only high value low bulk goods continued to be shipped in the transatlantic trade. It stimulated local production of foodstuffs, rough textiles, and other products for a mass market. Although New Spain produced considerable sugar and wheat, these were consumed exclusively in the colony even though there was demand elsewhere. Philadelphia, not New Spain, supplied Cuba with wheat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Caribbean port of Veracruz was small, with its hot, pestilential climate not a draw for permanent settlers. Its population never topped 10,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> Many Spanish merchants much preferred living in the pleasant highland town of Jalapa (1,500 m). For a brief period (1722–76) the town of Jalapa became even more important than Veracruz when it was granted the right to hold the royal trade fair for New Spain, serving as the entrepot for goods from Asia via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manila_Galleon" title="Manila Galleon">Manila Galleon</a> through the port of Acapulco and European goods via the flota (convoy) from the Spanish port of Cádiz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> Spaniards also settled in the temperate area of Orizaba, east of the Citlaltepetl volcano. Orizaba varied considerably in elevation from 800 metres (2,600 ft) to 5,700 metres (18,700 ft) (the summit of the Citlaltepetl volcano), but "most of the inhabited part is temperate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> Some Spaniards lived in semitropical Córdoba, which was founded as a villa in 1618, to serve as a Spanish base against runaway slave (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cimarr%C3%B3nes" title="Cimarrónes">cimarrón</a>) predations on mule trains traveling the route from the port to the capital. Some cimarron settlements sought autonomy, such as one led by <a href="/wiki/Gaspar_Yanga" title="Gaspar Yanga">Yanga</a>, with whom the crown concluded a treaty leading to the recognition of a largely black town, San Lorenzo de los Negros de Cerralvo, now called the municipality of Yanga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>European diseases immediately affected the multiethnic Indian populations in the Veracruz area and for that reason Spaniards imported black slaves as either an alternative to indigenous labor or its complete replacement in the event of a repetition of the Caribbean die-off. A few Spaniards acquired prime agricultural lands left vacant by the indigenous demographic disaster. Portions of the province could support sugar cultivation and as early as the 1530s sugar production was underway. New Spain's first viceroy, Don <a href="/wiki/Antonio_de_Mendoza" title="Antonio de Mendoza">Antonio de Mendoza</a> established an hacienda on lands taken from Orizaba.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Indians resisted cultivating sugarcane themselves, preferring to tend their subsistence crops. As in the Caribbean, black slave labor became crucial to the development of sugar estates. During the period 1580-1640 when Spain and Portugal were ruled by the same monarch and Portuguese slave traders had access to Spanish markets, African slaves were imported in large numbers to New Spain and many of them remained in the region of Veracruz. But even when that connection was broken and prices rose, black slaves remained an important component of Córdoba's labor sector even after 1700. Rural estates in Córdoba depended on African slave labor, who were 20% of the population there, a far greater proportion than any other area of New Spain, and greater than even nearby Jalapa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1765 the crown created a monopoly on tobacco, which directly affected agriculture and manufacturing the Veracruz region. Tobacco was a valuable, high demand product. Men, women, and even children smoked, something commented on by foreign travelers and depicted in eighteenth-century <a href="/wiki/Casta" title="Casta">casta</a> paintings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> The crown calculated that tobacco could produce a steady stream of tax revenues by supplying the huge Mexican demand, so the crown limited zones of tobacco cultivation. It also established a small number of manufactories of finished products, and licensed distribution outlets (<i>estanquillos</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> The crown also set up warehouses to store up to a year's worth of supplies, including paper for cigarettes, for the manufactories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> With the establishment of the monopoly, crown revenues increased and there is evidence that despite high prices and expanding rates of poverty, tobacco consumption rose while at the same time general consumption fell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1787 during the <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_Reforms" title="Bourbon Reforms">Bourbon Reforms</a> Veracruz became an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intendancy" title="Intendancy">intendancy</a>, a new administrative unit.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Valley_of_Puebla">Valley of Puebla</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Valley of Puebla">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Founded in 1531 as a Spanish settlement, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puebla_de_los_Angeles" title="Puebla de los Angeles">Puebla de los Angeles</a> quickly rose to the status of Mexico's second-most important city. Its location on the main route between the viceregal capital and the port of Veracruz, in a fertile basin with a dense indigenous population, largely not held in encomienda, made Puebla a destination for many later arriving Spaniards. If there had been significant mineral wealth in Puebla, it could have been even more prominent a center for New Spain, but its first century established its importance. In 1786 it became the capital of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intendancy" title="Intendancy">intendancy</a> of the same name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>It became the seat of the richest diocese in New Spain in its first century, with the seat of the first diocese, formerly in Tlaxcala, moved there in 1543.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> Bishop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Juan_de_Palafox" title="Juan de Palafox">Juan de Palafox</a> asserted the income from the diocese of Puebla as being twice that of the archbishopic of Mexico, due to the tithe income derived from agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> In its first hundred years, Puebla was prosperous from wheat farming and other agriculture, as the ample tithe income indicates, plus manufacturing woolen cloth for the domestic market. Merchants, manufacturers, and artisans were important to the city's economic fortunes, but its early prosperity was followed by stagnation and decline in the seventeenth and eighteenth centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>The foundation of the town of Puebla was a pragmatic social experiment to settle Spanish immigrants without encomiendas to pursue farming and industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirschberg_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hirschberg-70">[69]</a></sup> Puebla was privileged in a number of ways, starting with its status as a Spanish settlement not founded on existing indigenous city-state, but with a significant indigenous population. It was located in a fertile basin on a temperate plateau in the nexus of the key trade triangle of Veracruz–Mexico City–Antequera (Oaxaca). Although there were no encomiendas in Puebla itself, encomenderos with nearby labor grants settled in Puebla. And despite its foundation as a Spanish city, sixteenth-century Puebla had Indians resident in the central core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirschberg_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hirschberg-70">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Administratively Puebla was far enough away from Mexico City (approximately 100 miles) so as not to be under its direct influence. Puebla's Spanish town council (cabildo) had considerable autonomy and was not dominated by encomenderos. The administrative structure of Puebla "may be seen as a subtle expression of royal absolutism, the granting of extensive privileges to a town of commoners, amounting almost to republican self-government, in order to curtail the potential authority of encomenderos and the religious orders, as well as to counterbalance the power of the viceregal capital."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>The "golden century" from its founding in 1531 until the early 1600s saw Puebla's agricultural sector flourish, with small-scale Spanish farmers plowing the land for the first time, planting wheat and vaulting Puebla to importance as New Spain's breadbasket, a role assumed by the <a href="/wiki/Baj%C3%ADo" title="Bajío">Bajío</a> (including Querétaro) in the seventeenth century, and Guadalajara in the eighteenth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> Puebla's wheat production was the initial element of its prosperity, but it emerged as a manufacturing and commercial center, "serving as the inland port of Mexico's Atlantic trade."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> Economically, the city received exemptions from the <i>alcabala</i> (sales tax) and <i>almojarifazgo</i> (import/export duties) for its first century (1531–1630), which helped promote commerce.</p>
<p>Puebla built a significant manufacturing sector, mainly in textile production in workshops (obrajes), supplying New Spain and markets as far away as Guatemala and Peru. Transatlantic ties between a particular Spanish town, <a href="/wiki/Brihuega" title="Brihuega">Brihuega</a>, and Puebla demonstrate the close connection between the two settlements. The take-off for Puebla's manufacturing sector did not simply coincide with immigration from Brihuega, but was crucial to "shaping and driving Puebla's economic development, especially in the manufacturing sector."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> Brihuega immigrants not only came to Mexico with expertise in textile production, but the transplanted briocenses provided capital to create large-scale obrajes. Although obrajes in Brihuega were small-scale enterprises, quite a number of them in Puebla employed up to 100 workers. Supplies of wool, water for fulling mills, and labor (free indigenous, incarcerated Indians, black slaves) were available. Although much of Puebla's textile output was rough cloth, it also produced higher quality dyed cloth with <a href="/wiki/Cochineal" title="Cochineal">cochineal</a> from Oaxaca and <a href="/wiki/Indigo" title="Indigo">indigo</a> from <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> But by the eighteenth century, <a href="/wiki/Quer%C3%A9taro" title="Querétaro">Querétaro</a> had displaced Puebla as the mainstay of woolen textile production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1787, Puebla became an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intendancy" title="Intendancy">intendancy</a> as part of the new administrative structuring of the <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_Reforms" title="Bourbon Reforms">Bourbon Reforms</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Valley_of_Mexico">Valley of Mexico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Valley of Mexico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Mexico City dominated the Valley of Mexico, but the valley continued to have dense indigenous populations challenged by growing, increasingly dense Spanish settlement. The Valley of Mexico had many former Indian city-states that became Indian towns in the colonial era. These towns continued to be ruled by indigenous elites under the Spanish crown, with an indigenous governor and a town councils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> These Indian towns close to the capital were the most desirable ones for encomenderos to hold and for the friars to evangelize.</p>
<p>The capital was provisioned by the indigenous towns and its labor was available for enterprises that ultimately created a colonial economy. The gradual drying up of the central lake system created more dry land for farming, but the sixteenth-century population declines allowed Spaniards to expand their acquisition of land. One region that retained strong Indian land holding was the southern fresh water area, which important suppliers of fresh produce to the capital. The area was characterized by intensely cultivated chinampas, man-made extensions of cultivable land into the lake system. These chinampa towns retained a strong indigenous character and Indians continued to hold the majority of that land, despite its closeness to the Spanish capital. A key example is <a href="/wiki/Xochimilco" title="Xochimilco">Xochimilco</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Texcoco in the pre-conquest period had been one of the three members of the Aztec Triple Alliance and the cultural center of the empire fell on hard times in the colonial period as an economic backwater. Spaniards with any ambition or connections would be lured by the closeness of Mexico City, so that the Spanish presence was minimal and marginal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tlaxcala, the major ally of the Spanish against the Aztecs of Tenochtitlan, also became something of a backwater, but like Puebla it did not come under the control of Spanish encomenderos. No elite Spaniards settled there, but like many other Indian towns in the Valley of Mexico, it had an assortment of small-scale merchants, artisans, farmers and ranchers, and textile workshops (obrajes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_North">The North</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: The North">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since portions of northern New Spain became part of the United States' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southwest_United_States" title="Southwest United States">Southwest region</a>, there has been considerable scholarship on the Spanish borderlands in the north. The motor of the Spanish colonial economy was the extraction of <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, it was from the single rich mountain of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cerro_de_Potos%C3%AD" title="Cerro de Potosí">Potosí</a>; but in New Spain, there were two major mining sites, one in <a href="/wiki/Zacatecas" title="Zacatecas">Zacatecas</a>, the other in <a href="/wiki/Guanajuato" title="Guanajuato">Guanajuato</a>.</p>
<p>The region farther north of the main mining zones attracted few Spanish settlers. Where there were settled <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">indigenous populations</a>, such as in the present-day state of <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> and in coastal regions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baja_California_(region)" title="Baja California (region)">Baja</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alta_California" title="Alta California">Alta California</a>, indigenous culture retained considerable integrity.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Baj.C3.ADo.2C_Mexico.27s_Breadbasket">The Bajío, Mexico's Breadbasket</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: The Bajío, Mexico's Breadbasket">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Bajío, a rich, fertile lowland just north of central Mexico, was nonetheless a frontier region between the densely populated plateaus and valleys of Mexico's center and south and the harsh northern desert controlled by nomadic Chichimeca. Devoid of settled indigenous populations in the early sixteenth century, the Bajío did not initially attract Spaniards, who were much more interested in exploiting labor and collecting tribute whenever possible. The region did not have indigenous populations that practiced subsistence agriculture. The Bajío developed in the colonial period as a region of commercial agriculture.</p>
<p>The discovery of mining deposits in Zacatecas and Guanajuato in the mid-sixteenth century and later in San Luis Potosí stimulated the Bajío's development to supply the mines with food and livestock. A network of Spanish towns was established in this region of commercial agriculture, with Querétaro also becoming a center of textile production. Although there were no dense indigenous populations or network of settlements, Indians migrated to the Bajío to work as resident employees on the region's haciendas and ranchos or rented land (terrasguerros). From diverse cultural backgrounds and with no sustaining indigenous communities, these indios were quickly hispanized, but largely remained at the bottom of the economic hierarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> Although Indians migrated willingly to the region, they did so in such small numbers that labor shortages prompted Spanish hacendados to provide incentives to attract workers, especially in the initial boom period of the early seventeenth century. Land owners lent workers money, which could be seen as a perpetual indebtedness, but it can be seen not as coercing Indians to stay but a way estate owners sweetened their terms of employment, beyond their basic wage labor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> For example, in 1775 the Spanish administrator of a San Luis Potosí estate "had to scour both Mexico City and the northern towns to find enough blue French linen to satisfy the resident employees."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> Other types of goods they received on credit were textiles, hats, shoes, candles, meat, beans, and a guaranteed ration of maize. However, where labor was more abundant or market conditions depressed, estate owners paid lower wages. The more sparsely populated northern Bajío tended to pay higher wages than the southern Bajío, which was increasingly integrated in the economy of central Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tutino_1979_364_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tutino_1979_364-88">[87]</a></sup> The credit-based employment system often privileged those holding higher ranked positions on the estate (supervisors, craftsmen, other specialists) who were mostly white, and the estates did not demand repayment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the late colonial period, renting complemented estate employment for many non-Indians in more central areas of the Bajío with access to markets. As with hacendados, renters produced for the commercial market. While these Bajío renters could prosper in good times and achieved a level of independence, drought and other disasters made their choice more risky than beneficial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many renters retained ties to the estates, diversifying their household's sources of income and level of economic security. In San Luis Potosí, rentals were fewer and estate employment the norm. After a number of years of drought and bad harvests in the first decade of the nineteenth century Hidalgo's 1810 grito appealed more in the Bajío than in San Luis Potosí. In the Bajío estate owners were evicting tenants in favor of renters better able to pay more for land, there was a disruption of previous patterns of mutual benefit between estate owners and renters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tutino_1979_364_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tutino_1979_364-88">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_Borderlands">Spanish Borderlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Spanish Borderlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Areas of northern Mexico were incorporated into the United States in the mid-nineteenth century, following Texas independence and the Mexican–American War (1846–48) and generally known as the "Spanish Borderlands".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> Scholars in the United States have extensively studied this northern region, which became the states of Texas, New Mexico, Arizona, and California.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> During the period of Spanish rule, this area was sparsely populated even by indigenous peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Presidio" title="Presidio">Presidios</a> (forts), <a href="/wiki/Pueblo" title="Pueblo">pueblos</a> (civilian towns) and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Spanish_missions" title="List of Spanish missions">misiones</a> (missions) were the three major agencies employed by the Spanish crown to extend its borders and consolidate its colonial holdings in these territories.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Missions_and_the_Northern_Frontier">Missions and the Northern Frontier</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Missions and the Northern Frontier">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albuquerque" title="Albuquerque">Albuquerque</a> (present day Albuquerque, <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>) was founded in 1706. The Mexican towns of: Paso del Norte (present day <a href="/wiki/Ciudad_Ju%C3%A1rez" title="Ciudad Juárez">Ciudad Juárez</a>) founded in 1667; Santiago de la <a href="/wiki/Monclova" title="Monclova">Monclova</a> in 1689; Panzacola, Tejas in 1681; and San Francisco de Cuéllar (present day city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chihuahua,_Chihuahua" title="Chihuahua, Chihuahua">Chihuahua</a>) in 1709. From 1687, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Father_Kino" title="Father Kino">Father Eusebio Francisco Kino</a>, with funding from the Marqués de Villapuente, founded over twenty <a href="/wiki/Spanish_missions_in_the_Sonoran_Desert" title="Spanish missions in the Sonoran Desert">missions</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sonoran_Desert" title="Sonoran Desert">Sonoran Desert</a> (in present-day <a href="/wiki/Sonora" title="Sonora">Sonora</a> and Arizona). From 1697, Jesuits established eighteen <a href="/wiki/Spanish_missions_in_California#Missions_in_present-day_Baja_California_.28Mexico.29" title="Spanish missions in California">missions throughout the Baja California Peninsula</a>. Between 1687 and 1700 several <a href="/wiki/Spanish_missions_in_Trinidad" title="Spanish missions in Trinidad">Missions were founded in Trinidad</a>, but only four survived as Amerindian villages throughout the 18th century. In 1691, explorers and missionaries visited the interior of Texas and came upon a river and Amerindian settlement on June 13, the feast day of <a href="/wiki/Anthony_of_Padua" title="Anthony of Padua">St. Anthony</a>, and named the location and river <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Antonio,_Texas" title="San Antonio, Texas">San Antonio</a> in his honor.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WLA_lacma_1690_folding_screen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="569" data-file-width="999" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/WLA_lacma_1690_folding_screen.jpg/260px-WLA_lacma_1690_folding_screen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/WLA_lacma_1690_folding_screen.jpg/390px-WLA_lacma_1690_folding_screen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/WLA_lacma_1690_folding_screen.jpg/520px-WLA_lacma_1690_folding_screen.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
