<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Mexico">New Mexico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: New Mexico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During the term of <a href="/wiki/Viceroy" title="Viceroy">viceroy</a> Don <a href="/wiki/Luis_de_Velasco,_marqu%C3%A9s_de_Salinas" title="Luis de Velasco, marqués de Salinas">Luis de Velasco, marqués de Salinas</a> the crown ended the long-running <a href="/wiki/Chichimeca_War" title="Chichimeca War">Chichimeca War</a> by making peace with the semi-nomadic <a href="/wiki/Chichimeca" title="Chichimeca">Chichimeca</a> indigenous tribes of northern México in 1591. This allowed expansion into the 'Province of New Mexico' or <i><a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe_de_Nuevo_M%C3%A9xico" title="Santa Fe de Nuevo México">Provincia de Nuevo México</a></i>. In 1595, Don <a href="/wiki/Juan_de_O%C3%B1ate" title="Juan de Oñate">Juan de Oñate</a>, son of one the key figures in the silver remining region of Zacatecas, received official permission from the viceroy to explore and conquer New Mexico. As was the pattern of such expeditions, the leader assumed the greatest risk but would reap the largest rewards, so that Oñate would become <i>capitán general</i> of New Mexico and had the authority to distribute rewards to those in the expedition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> Oñate pioneered 'The Royal Road of the Interior Land' or <i><a href="/wiki/El_Camino_Real_de_Tierra_Adentro" title="El Camino Real de Tierra Adentro">El Camino Real de Tierra Adentro</a></i> between <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tewa_people" title="Tewa people">Tewa</a> village of <a href="/wiki/Ohkay_Owingeh,_New_Mexico" title="Ohkay Owingeh, New Mexico">Ohkay Owingeh</a>, or San Juan Pueblo. He also founded the Spanish settlement of <a href="/wiki/San_Gabriel_de_Yungue-Ouinge" title="San Gabriel de Yungue-Ouinge">San Gabriel de Yungue-Ouinge</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande" title="Rio Grande">Rio Grande</a> near the <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> Pueblo, located just north of the present day city of <a href="/wiki/Espa%C3%B1ola,_New_Mexico" title="Española, New Mexico">Española, New Mexico</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>By naming the region "New Mexico", the Spanish likely hoped to incorporate a region as rich as Mexico had proven to be. However, while New Mexico had a settled indigenous population, there were no silver mines, little arable land, and few other resources to exploit that would merit large scale colonization. Oñate resigned as governor in 1607 and left New Mexico, having spent much of his personal wealth on the enterprise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1610, <a href="/wiki/Pedro_de_Peralta" title="Pedro de Peralta">Pedro de Peralta</a>, a later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_governors_of_New_Mexico" title="Spanish governors of New Mexico">governor of the Province of New Mexico</a>, established the settlement of <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe,_New_Mexico" title="Santa Fe, New Mexico">Santa Fe</a> near the southern end of the <a href="/wiki/Sangre_de_Cristo_Mountains" title="Sangre de Cristo Mountains">Sangre de Cristo</a> mountain range. <a href="/wiki/List_of_Spanish_missions" title="List of Spanish missions">Missions</a> were established to convert the locals, and manage the agricultural industry. The territory's <a href="/wiki/Puebloan_peoples" title="Puebloan peoples">indigenous population</a> resented the Spanish prohibition of their traditional religion, and the <a href="/wiki/Encomienda" title="Encomienda">encomienda</a> system of forced labor. The unrest led to the <a href="/wiki/Pueblo_Revolt" title="Pueblo Revolt">Pueblo Revolt</a> in 1680, forcing the Spanish to retreat to Paso del Norte (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Ciudad_Ju%C3%A1rez" title="Ciudad Juárez">Ciudad Juárez</a>.) After the return of the Spanish in 1692, the final resolution included a marked reduction of Spanish efforts to eradicate native culture and religion, the issuing of substantial communal <a href="/wiki/Land_grant" title="Land grant">land grants</a> to each Pueblo, and a public defender of their rights and for their legal cases in Spanish courts. In 1776 the Province came under the new <a href="/wiki/Provincias_Internas" title="Provincias Internas">Provincias Internas</a> jurisdiction. In the late 18th century the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_land_grants_in_New_Mexico" title="Spanish land grants in New Mexico">Spanish land grant</a> encouraged the settlement by individuals of large land parcels outside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mission_(Christian)" title="Mission (Christian)">Mission</a> and Pueblo boundaries, many of which became ranchos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="California">California</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: California">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1602, <a href="/wiki/Sebasti%C3%A1n_Vizca%C3%ADno" title="Sebastián Vizcaíno">Sebastián Vizcaíno</a>, the first Spanish presence in the 'New California' (<i>Nueva California</i>) region of the frontier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Las_Californias" title="Las Californias">Las Californias</a> province since Cabrillo in 1542, sailed as far north up the <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="West Coast of the United States">Pacific Coast</a> as present-day <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, and named California coastal features from <a href="/wiki/San_Diego" title="San Diego">San Diego</a> to as far north as <a href="/wiki/Monterey_Bay" title="Monterey Bay">the Bay of Monterrey</a>.</p>
<p>Not until the eighteenth century was California of much interest to the Spanish crown, since it had no known rich mineral deposits or indigenous populations sufficiently organized to render tribute and do labor for Spaniards. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/California_gold_rush" title="California gold rush">discovery of huge deposits of gold</a> in the Sierra Nevada foothills did not come until after the U.S. had incorporated California following the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican–American War</a> (1846–48).</p>
<p>By the middle of the 1700s, the Catholic order of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuits" title="Jesuits">Jesuits</a> had established a number of missions on the <a href="/wiki/Baja_California_Peninsula" title="Baja California Peninsula"><i>Baja</i> (lower) California peninsula</a>. Then, in 1767, <a href="/wiki/Charles_III_of_Spain" title="Charles III of Spain">King Charles III</a> ordered all Jesuits expelled from all Spanish possessions, including New Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> New Spain's Visitador General <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_de_G%C3%A1lvez" title="José de Gálvez">José de Gálvez</a> replaced them with the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican Order</a> in Baja California, and the <a href="/wiki/Franciscans" title="Franciscans">Franciscans</a> were chosen to establish new northern missions in <a href="/wiki/Alta_California" title="Alta California"><i>Alta</i> (upper) California</a>.</p>
<p>In 1768, Gálvez received the following orders: "Occupy and fortify San Diego and Monterey for God and the King of Spain." The <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spanish</a> colonization there, with far fewer known natural resources and less cultural development than Mexico or Peru, was to combine establishing a presence for defense of the territory with a perceived responsibility to convert the indigenous people to Christianity.</p>
<p>The method used to "occupy and fortify" was the established Spanish colonial system: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_Missions_of_California" title="Spanish Missions of California">missions</a> (<i>misiones</i>, between 1769 and 1833 twenty-one missions were established) aimed at converting the indigenes to Christianity, <a href="/wiki/Presidio" title="Presidio">forts</a> (<i>presidios</i>, four total) to protect the missionaries, and secular <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Pueblo_(disambiguation)" title="Pueblo (disambiguation)">municipalities</a> (<i>pueblos</i>, three total). Due to the region's great distance from supplies and support in México, the system had to be largely self-sufficient. As a result, the colonial population of California remained small, widely scattered and near the coast.</p>
<p>In 1776, the north-western frontier areas came under the administration of the new 'Commandancy General of the Internal Provinces of the North' <i>(<a href="/wiki/Provincias_Internas" title="Provincias Internas">Provincias Internas</a>)</i>, designed to streamline administration and invigorate growth. The crown created two new provincial governments from the former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Las_Californias" title="Las Californias">Las Californias</a> in 1804; the southern peninsula became Baja California, and the ill-defined northern mainland frontier area became Alta California.</p>
<p>Once missions and protective presidios were established in an area, large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_land_grants_in_California" title="Spanish land grants in California">land grants</a> encouraged settlement and establishment of <a href="/wiki/Ranchos_of_California" title="Ranchos of California">California ranchos</a>. The Spanish system of land grants was not very successful, however, because the grants were merely royal concessions—not actual land ownership. Under later Mexican rule, land grants conveyed ownership, and were more successful at promoting settlement.</p>
<p>Rancho activities centered on cattle-raising; many grantees emulated the <a href="/wiki/Don_(honorific)" title="Don (honorific)">Dons</a> of <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, with cattle, horses and sheep the source of wealth. The work was usually done by <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans</a>, sometimes displaced and/or relocated from their villages. Native-born descendants of the resident Spanish-heritage rancho grantees, soldiers, servants, merchants, craftsmen and others became the <a href="/wiki/Californio" title="Californio">Californios</a>. Many of the less-affluent men took native wives, and many daughters married later English, French and American settlers.</p>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_War_of_Independence" title="Mexican War of Independence">Mexican War of Independence</a> (1821) and subsequent <a href="/wiki/Secularization" title="Secularization">secularization</a> ("disestablishment") of the missions (1834), Mexican land grant transactions increased the spread of the rancho system. The land grants and ranchos established mapping and land-ownership patterns that are still recognizable in present-day California and New Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_South">The South</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: The South">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Yucat.C3.A1n">Yucatán</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Yucatán">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Yucatán peninsula can be seen as a cul-de-sac,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> and it does indeed have unique features, but it also has strong similarities to other areas in the South. The Yucatán peninsula extends into the Gulf of Mexico and was connected to Caribbean trade routes and Mexico City, far more than some other southern regions, such as Oaxaca.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunt_59_60_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt_59_60-105">[104]</a></sup> There was three main Spanish settlements, the inland city of Mérida, where Spanish civil and religious officials had their headquarters and where the many Spaniards in the province lived. The <i>villa</i> of Campeche was the peninsula's port, the key gateway for the whole region. A merchant group developed and expanded dramatically as trade flourished during the seventeenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> Although that period was once characterized as New Spain's "century of depression", for Yucatán this was certainly not the case, with sustained growth from the early seventeenth century to the end of the colonial period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>With dense indigenous Maya populations, Yucatán's <a href="/wiki/Encomienda" title="Encomienda">encomienda</a> system was established early and persisted far longer than in central Mexico, since fewer Spaniards migrated to the region than in the center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> Although Yucatán was a more peripheral area to the colony, since it lacked rich mining areas and no agricultural or other export product, it did have complex of Spanish settlement, with a whole range of social types in the main settlements of Mérida and the villas of Campeche and Valladolid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> There was an important sector of mixed-race <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Castas" title="Castas">castas</a>, some of whom were fully at home in both the indigenous and Hispanic worlds. Blacks were an important component of Yucatecan society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> The largest population in the province was indigenous Maya, who lived in their communities, but which were in contact with the Hispanic sphere via labor demands and commerce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Yucatán Spanish rule was largely indirect, allowing these communities considerable political and cultural autonomy. The Maya community, the <i>cah</i>, was the means by which indigenous cultural integrity was maintained. In the economic sphere, unlike many other regions and ethnic groups in Mesoamerica, the Yucatec Maya did not have a pre-conquest network of regular markets to exchange different types of food and craft goods. Perhaps because the peninsula was uniform in its ecosystem local niche production did not develop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> Production of cotton textiles, largely by Maya women, helped pay households' tribute obligations, but basic crops were the basis of the economy. The cah retained considerable land under the control of religious brotherhoods or confraternities (<i>cofradías</i>), the device by which Maya communities avoided colonial officials, the clergy, or even indigenous rulers (<i>gobernadores</i>) from diverting of community revenues in their <i>cajas de comunidad</i> (literally community-owned chests that had locks and keys). Cofradías were traditionally lay pious organizations and burial societies, but in Yucatán they became significant holders of land, a source of revenue for pious purposes kept under cah control. "[I]n Yucatán the cofradía in its modified form was the community".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup> Local Spanish clergy had no reason to object to the arrangement since much of the revenue went for payment for masses or other spiritual matters controlled by the priest.</p>
<p>A limiting factor in Yucatán's economy was the poorness of the limestone soil, which could only support crops for two to three years with land cleared through slash and burn (swidden) agriculture. Access to water was a limiting factor on agriculture, with the limestone escarpment giving way in water filled sinkholes (<i>cenotes</i>), but rivers and streams were generally absent on the peninsula. Individuals had rights to land so long as they cleared and tilled them and when the soil was exhausted, they repeated the process. In general Indians lived in a dispersed pattern, which Spanish <i>congregación</i> or forced resettlement attempted to alter. Collective labor cultivated the confraternities' lands, which included raising the traditional maize, beans, and cotton. But confraternities also later pursued cattle ranching, as well as mule and horse breeding, depending on the local situation. There is evidence that cofradías in southern Campeche were involved in interregional trade in cacao as well as cattle ranching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> Although generally the revenues from crops and animals were devoted to expenses in the spiritual sphere, cofradías' cattle were used for direct aid to community members during droughts, stabilizing the community's food supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the seventeenth century, patterns shifted in Yucatán and Tabasco, as the English took territory the Spanish claimed but did not control, especially what became British Honduras (now Belize), where they cut dyewood and in Laguna de Términos (Isla del Carmen) where they cut logwood. In 1716–17 viceroy of New Spain organized a sufficient ships to expel the foreigners, where the crown subsequently built a fortress at Isla del Carmen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup> But the British held onto their territory in the eastern portion of the peninsula into the twentieth century. In the nineteenth century, the enclave supplied guns to the rebellious Maya in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caste_War_of_Yucatan" title="Caste War of Yucatan">Caste War of Yucatan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Valley_of_Oaxaca">Valley of Oaxaca</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Valley of Oaxaca">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Since Oaxaca was lacking in mineral deposits and it had an abundant sedentary indigenous population, its development was notable for the lack of European or mixed-race population, lack of large-scale Spanish haciendas, and the survival of indigenous communities. These communities retained their land, indigenous languages, and distinct ethnic identities. Antequera (now Oaxaca City) was a Spanish settlement founded in 1529, but the rest of Oaxaca consisted of indigenous towns. Despite its remoteness from Mexico City "throughout the colonial era, Oaxaca was one of Mexico's most prosperous provinces."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[Note 2]</a></sup> In the eighteenth century, the value of crown offices (alcalde mayor or corregidor) were the highest for two Oaxaca jurisdictions, with Jicayan and Villa Alta each worth 7,500 pesos, Cuicatlan-Papalotipac, 4,500; Teposcolula and Chichicapa each 4,200 pesos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[Note 3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most important commodity for Oaxaca was <a href="/wiki/Cochineal" title="Cochineal">cochineal</a> red dye. Cochineal's commodity chain is an interesting one, with indigenous peasants in the remote areas of Oaxaca ultimately linked to Amsterdam and London commodity exchanges and the European production of luxury cloth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[119]</a></sup> The most extensive scholarly work on Oaxaca's eighteenth-century economy deals with the nexus between the local crown officials (alcaldes mayores), merchant investors (<i>aviadores</i>), the repartimiento (forced labor), and indigenous products, particularly cochineal. The rich, color-fast red dye produced from insects, was harvested from nopal cacti. Cochineal was a high value, low volume product that became the second most valuable Mexican export after silver. Although it could be produced elsewhere in central and southern Mexico, its main region of production was Oaxaca. For the indigenous in Oaxaca, cochineal was the only one "with which the [tributaries] maintain themselves and pay their debts" but it also had other advantages for them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[Note 4]</a></sup> Producing cochineal was time-consuming labor, but it was not particularly difficult and could be done by the elderly, women, and children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[121]</a></sup> It was also important to households and communities because it initially did not require the indigenous to displace their existing crops or migrate elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the repartimiento has historically been seen as an imposition on the indigenous, forcing them into economic relations they would rather have avoided and maintained by force,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[123]</a></sup> recent work on eighteenth-century Oaxaca analyzes the nexus of crown officials (the alcaldes mayores) and Spanish merchants, and indigenous via the repartimiento. cash loaned by local crown officials (the alcalde mayor and his teniente), usually to individual Indians but sometimes to communities, in exchange for a fixed amount of a good (cochineal or cotton mantles) at a later date. Indigenous elites were an integral part of the repartimiento, often being recipients of large extensions of credit. As authority figures in their community, they were in a good position to collect on the debt, the most risky part of the business from the Spanish point of view.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tehuantepec">Tehuantepec</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Tehuantepec">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Isthmus of <a href="/wiki/Tehuantepec" title="Tehuantepec">Tehuantepec</a> region of Oaxaca was important for its short transit between the Gulf Coast and the Pacific, facilitating both overland and sea trade. The province of Tehuantepec was the Pacific side of the isthmus and the headwaters of the Coatzacoalcos River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerhard_264_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerhard_264-128">[124]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hern%C3%A1n_Cort%C3%A9s" title="Hernán Cortés">Hernán Cortés</a> acquired strategically located holdings entailed in the Marquesado including Huatulco,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[Note 5]</a></sup> once the main Pacific Coast port before <a href="/wiki/Acapulco" title="Acapulco">Acapulco</a> replaced it in 1563.</p>
<p>Gold mining was an early draw for Spaniards, who directed indigenous labor to its extraction, but did not continue beyond the mid-sixteenth century. Over the long run, ranching and commerce were the most important economic activities, with the settlement of Tehuantepec becoming the hub. The region's history can be divided into three distinct periods, an initial period of engagement with Spanish colonial rule to 1563, during which there was a working relationship with the Zapotec ruling line and the establishment of Cortés's economic enterprises. This early period came to a close with the death of the last native king in 1562 and the escheatment of Cortés's Tehuantepec encomiendas to the crown in 1563. The second period of approximately a century (1563–1660) saw the decline of the indigenous entailed estate (<i>cacicazgo</i>) and indigenous political power and development of the colonial economy and imposition of Spanish political and religious structures. The final period is the maturation of these structures (1660–1750). The 1660 rebellion can be a dividing line between the two later periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Villa of Tehuantepec, the largest settlement on the isthmus, was an important prehispanic Zapotec trade and religious center, which was not under the jurisdiction of the Aztecs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerhard_264_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gerhard_264-128">[124]</a></sup> The early colonial history of Tehuantepec and the larger province was dominated by Cortés and the Marquesado, but the crown realized the importance of the area and concluded an agreement in 1563 with the second Marqués by which the crown took control of the Tehuantepec encomienda. The Marquesado continued to have major private holdings in the province. The Villa of Tehuantepec became a center of Spanish and mixed-race settlement, crown administration, and trade.</p>
<p>The Cortés haciendas in Tehuantepec were key components of the province's economy, and they were directly linked to other Marquesado enterprises in greater Mexico in an integrated fashion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[127]</a></sup> The Dominicans also had significant holdings in Tehuantepec, but there has been little research on these. However important the Marquesado and the Dominican enterprises were, there were also other economic players in the region, including individual Spaniards as well as existing indigenous communities. Ranching emerged as the dominant rural enterprise in most of Tehuantepec with a ranching boom in the period 1580–1640. Since Tehuantepec experienced significant indigenous population loss in the sixteenth century conforming to the general pattern, ranching made possible for Spaniards to thrive in Tehuantepec because ranching was not dependent on significant amounts of indigenous labor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeitlin_1989_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zeitlin_1989-133">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most detailed economic records for the region are of the Marquesado's ranching haciendas, which produced draft animals (horses, mules, burros, and oxen) and sheep and goats, for meat and wool. Cattle ranching for meat, tallow, and leather were also important. Tallow for candles used in churches and residences and leather used in a variety of ways (saddles, other tack, boots, furniture, machinery) were significant items in the larger colonial economy, finding markets well beyond Tehuantepec. Since the Marquesado operated as an integrated enterprise, draft animals were used in other holdings for transport, agriculture, and mining in Oaxaca, Morelos, Toluca, and Mexico City as well as sold. Raised in Tehuantepec, the animals were driven to other Marquesado holdings for use and distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although colonial population decline affected the indigenous in Tehuantepec, their communities remained important in the colonial era and remain distinctly Indian to the current era. There were differences in the three distinct linguistic and ethnic groups in colonial Tehuantepec, the Zapotec, the Zoque, and the Huave. The Zapotecs concluded an alliance with the Spaniards at contact, and they had already expanded their territory into Zoque and Huave regions.</p>
<p>Under Spanish rule, the Zapotecs not only survived, but flourished, unlike the other two. They continued to pursue agriculture, some of it irrigated, which was not disrupted by the growing ranching economy. Generally Zapotec elites protected their communities from Spanish incursions and community cohesion remained strong as shown in members' performance of regular community service for social ends. Zapotec elites engaged in the market economy early on, which undermined to an extent the bonds between commoners and elites who colluded with the Spanish. In contrast to the Zapotecs, the Zoque generally declined as a group during the ranching boom, with interloping animals eating their maize crops. Zoque response was to take up being vaqueros themselves. They had access to the trade to Guatemala. Of the three indigenous groups, the Huave were the most isolated from the Spanish ranching economy and labor demands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[130]</a></sup> With little arable or grazing land, they exploited the lagoons of the Pacific coast, using shore and beach resources. They traded dried shrimp and fish, as well as purple dye from shells to Oaxaca, likely acquiring foodstuffs that they were unable to cultivate themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeitlin_1989_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zeitlin_1989-133">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>Not well documented is the number of African slaves and their descendants, who were artisans in urban areas and did hard manual labor in rural areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[131]</a></sup> In a pattern recognizable elsewhere, coastal populations were mainly African, including an unknown number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cimarr%C3%B3nes" title="Cimarrónes">cimarrón</a> (runaway slave) settlements, while inland indigenous communities were more prominent. On the Cortés haciendas, blacks and mulattoes were essential to the profitability of the enterprises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>In general, Tehuantepec was not a site of major historical events, but in 1660–61 there was a significant rebellion stemming from increased repartimiento Spanish demands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[133]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_role_of_epidemics">The role of epidemics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: The role of epidemics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Spanish settlers brought to the American continent <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a>, and other diseases. Most of the Spanish settlers had developed an immunity to these diseases from childhood, but the indigenous peoples lacked the needed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibodies" title="Antibodies">antibodies</a> since these diseases were totally alien to the native population at the time. There were at least three, separate, major epidemics that decimated the population: smallpox (1520 to 1521), measles (1545 to 1548) and typhus (1576 to 1581). In the course of the 16th century, the native population in Mexico went from an estimated pre-Columbian population of 8 to 20 million to less than two million. Therefore, at the start of the 17th century, continental New Spain was a depopulated country with abandoned cities and <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> fields. These diseases would not affect the Philippines in the same way because the diseases were already present in the country; Pre-Hispanic Filipinos had contact with other foreign nationalities before the arrival of the Spaniards.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_role_of_interracial_mixing">The role of interracial mixing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: The role of interracial mixing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Casta" title="Casta">Casta</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cabrera_Pintura_de_Castas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="484" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Cabrera_Pintura_de_Castas.jpg/170px-Cabrera_Pintura_de_Castas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Cabrera_Pintura_de_Castas.jpg/255px-Cabrera_Pintura_de_Castas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Cabrera_Pintura_de_Castas.jpg/340px-Cabrera_Pintura_de_Castas.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cabrera_Pintura_de_Castas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Español</i> and <i>Mulata</i> with their <i>Morisco</i> children.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the Spanish conquests, new ethnic groups were created, primary among them the <a href="/wiki/Mestizo" title="Mestizo">Mestizo</a>. The Mestizo population emerged as a result of the Spanish colonizers having children with indigenous women, both within and outside of wedlock, which brought about the mixing of both cultures. Many of the Spanish colonists were men with no wives and took partners from the indigenous population.</p>
<p>Initially, if a child was born in wedlock, the child was considered, and raised as, a member of the prominent parent's ethnicity. (<i>See <a href="/wiki/Hyperdescent" title="Hyperdescent">Hyperdescent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hypodescent" title="Hypodescent">Hypodescent</a>.</i>) Because of this, the term "Mestizo" was associated with illegitimacy. Mestizos do not appear in large numbers in official censuses until the second half of the 17th century, when a sizable and stable community of mixed-race people with no claims to being either Indian or Spanish appeared, although, of course, a large population of biological Mestizos had already existed for over a century in Mexico.</p>
<p>The Spanish conquest also brought the migration of people of African descent to the many regions of the Viceroyalty. Some came as free blacks, but vast majority came because of the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">African slavery</a>. As the native population was decimated by epidemics and forced labor, black slaves were imported. Mixes with Europeans and indigenous peoples also occurred, resulting in the creation of new racial categories such as <a href="/wiki/Mulatto" title="Mulatto">Mulattos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zambo" title="Zambo">Zambos</a> to account for these offspring. As with the term Mestizo, these other terms were associated with illegitimacy, since a majority—though not all—of these people were born outside of wedlock.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cabrera_15_Coyote.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="579" data-file-width="450" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Cabrera_15_Coyote.jpg/170px-Cabrera_15_Coyote.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Cabrera_15_Coyote.jpg/255px-Cabrera_15_Coyote.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Cabrera_15_Coyote.jpg/340px-Cabrera_15_Coyote.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cabrera_15_Coyote.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Mestizo</i> and <i>India</i> with their <i>Coyote</i> children.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Eventually a <a href="/wiki/Caste" title="Caste">caste</a> system was created to describe the various mixes and to assign them a different social level. In theory, each different mix had a name and different sets of privileges or prohibitions. In reality, mixed-race people were able to negotiate various racial and ethnic identities (often several ones at different points in their lives) depending on the family ties and wealth they had. In its general outline, the system reflected reality. The upper echelons of government were staffed by Spaniards born in Spain (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peninsulares" title="Peninsulares">peninsulares</a></i>), the middle and lower levels of government and other higher paying jobs were held by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Criollo_(people)" title="Criollo (people)">Criollos</a> (Criollos were Spaniards born in the Americas, or—as permitted by the <i>casta</i> system—Spaniards with some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amerindian" title="Amerindian">Amerindian</a> or even other ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[134]</a></sup>) The best lands were owned by Peninsulares and Criollos, with Native communities for the most part relegated to marginal lands. Mestizos and Mulattos held <a href="/wiki/Artisan" title="Artisan">artisanal</a> positions and unskilled laborers were either more mixed people, such as <a href="/wiki/Zambo" title="Zambo">Zambos</a>, recently freed slaves or Natives who had left their communities and settled in areas with large <a href="/wiki/Hispanic" title="Hispanic">Hispanic</a> populations. Native populations tended to have their own legally recognized communities (the <i>repúblicas de indios</i>) with their own social and economic hierarchies. This rough sketch must be complicated by the fact that not only did exceptions exist, but also that all these "racial" categories represented social conventions, as demonstrated by the fact that many persons were assigned a caste based on hyperdescent or hypodescent.</p>
<p>Even if mixes were common, the white population tried to keep their higher status, and were largely successful in doing so. With Mexican and Central American independence, the caste system and slavery were theoretically abolished. However, it can be argued that the Criollos simply replaced the Peninsulares in terms of power. Thus, for example, in modern Mexico, while Mestizos no longer have a separate legal status from other groups, they comprise approximately 65% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mexico-People_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mexico-People-140">[135]</a></sup> White people, who also no longer have a special legal status, are thought to be about 9–18% of the population,.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mexico-People_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mexico-People-140">[135]</a></sup> In modern Mexico, "Mestizo" has become more a cultural term, since Indigenous people who abandon their traditional ways are considered Mestizos. Also, most Afro-Mexicans prefer to be considered Mestizo, since they identify closely with this group. (<i>See also, <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Mexico" title="Demographics of Mexico">Demographics of Mexico</a>.</i>)</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_population_of_New_Spain_in_1810">The population of New Spain in 1810</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: The population of New Spain in 1810">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Population estimates from the first decade of the 19th century varied between 6,122,354 as calculated by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Francisco_Navarro_y_Noriega&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Francisco Navarro y Noriega (page does not exist)">Francisco Navarro y Noriega</a> in 1810,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[136]</a></sup> to 6.5 million as figured by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt" title="Alexander von Humboldt">Alexander von Humboldt</a> in 1808.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[137]</a></sup> Navarro y Noriega figured that half of his estimate constituted indigenous peoples. More recent data suggests that the actual population of New Spain in 1810 was closer to 5 or 5.5 million individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p><br/></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture.2C_art.2C_and_architecture">Culture, art, and architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Culture, art, and architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mexican_art" title="Mexican art">Mexican art</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_architecture" title="Mexican architecture">Mexican architecture</a></div>
<p>The capital of Viceroyalty of New Spain, Mexico City, was one of the principal centers of European cultural expansion in the Americas. Some of the most important early buildings in New Spain were churches and other religious architecture. Civil architecture included the viceregal palace, now the National Palace, and the Mexico City town council (<i>cabildo</i>), both located on the main square in the capital.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/House_of_the_First_Print_Shop_in_the_Americas" title="House of the First Print Shop in the Americas">first printing press in the New World</a> was brought to Mexico in 1539, by printer <a href="/wiki/Juan_Pablos" title="Juan Pablos">Juan Pablos</a> (Giovanni Paoli). The first book printed in Mexico was entitled <i>La escala espiritual de San Juan Clímaco</i>. In 1568, <a href="/wiki/Bernal_D%C3%ADaz_del_Castillo" title="Bernal Díaz del Castillo">Bernal Díaz del Castillo</a> finished <i>La Historia Verdadera de la Conquista de la Nueva España</i>. Figures such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sor_Juana_In%C3%A9s_de_la_Cruz" title="Sor Juana Inés de la Cruz">Sor Juana Inés de la Cruz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Juan_Ruiz_de_Alarc%C3%B3n" title="Juan Ruiz de Alarcón">Juan Ruiz de Alarcón</a>, and don <a href="/wiki/Carlos_de_Sig%C3%BCenza_y_G%C3%B3ngora" title="Carlos de Sigüenza y Góngora">Carlos de Sigüenza y Góngora</a>, stand out as some of the viceroyalty's most notable contributors to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_Literature" title="Spanish Literature">Spanish Literature</a>. In 1693, Sigüenza y Góngora published <i>El Mercurio Volante</i>, the first newspaper in New Spain.</p>
<p>Architects Pedro Martínez Vázquez and Lorenzo Rodriguez produced some fantastically extravagant and visually frenetic architecture known as <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture#Spanish_America" title="Baroque architecture">Mexican Churrigueresque</a> in the capital, <a href="/wiki/Ocotl%C3%A1n,_Tlaxcala" title="Ocotlán, Tlaxcala">Ocotlan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puebla,_Puebla" title="Puebla, Puebla">Puebla</a> or remote silver-mining towns. Composers including <a href="/wiki/Manuel_de_Zumaya" title="Manuel de Zumaya">Manuel de Zumaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Juan_Guti%C3%A9rrez_de_Padilla" title="Juan Gutiérrez de Padilla">Juan Gutiérrez de Padilla</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antonio_de_Salazar_(composer)" title="Antonio de Salazar (composer)">Antonio de Salazar</a> were active from the early 1500s through the <a href="/wiki/Baroque_music" title="Baroque music">Baroque period of music</a>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 21em; -webkit-column-width: 21em; column-width: 21em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHaring1947">Haring (1947)</a></span></li>

<li><cite class="citation book" id="CITEREFVan_Young1992">Van Young, Eric (1992). "Are Regions Good to Think?". In Eric Van Young. <i>Mexico's Regions</i>. Center for U.S.-Mexican Studies, UCSD.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANew+Spain&amp;rft.atitle=Are+Regions+Good+to+Think%3F&amp;rft.aufirst=Eric&amp;rft.aulast=Van+Young&amp;rft.btitle=Mexico%27s+Regions&amp;rft.date=1992&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.pub=Center+for+U.S.-Mexican+Studies%2C+UCSD&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
